<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Scramble for Africa</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Invasion, occupation, colonization, and annexation of Africa by European colonial powers</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For information on the colonisation of Africa prior to the 1880s, including Carthaginian and early European colonisation, see <a href="/wiki/Colonisation_of_Africa" title="Colonisation of Africa">Colonisation of Africa</a>. For the book by Thomas Pakenham, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Scramble_for_Africa" title="The Scramble for Africa">The Scramble for Africa</a>.</div>

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1295" data-file-width="1390" decoding="async" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg/300px-Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg/450px-Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg/600px-Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Areas of Africa controlled by <a href="/wiki/History_of_colonialism" title="History of colonialism">European colonial powers</a> in 1913 (<a href="/wiki/Belgian_colonial_empire" title="Belgian colonial empire">Belgian</a> (yellow), <a href="/wiki/British_Empire#Cape_to_Cairo" title="British Empire">British</a> (pink), <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">French</a> (blue), <a href="/wiki/German_colonial_empire" title="German colonial empire">German</a> (turquoise), <a href="/wiki/Italian_Empire" title="Italian Empire">Italian</a> (green), <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese</a> (purple), and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish</a> (lilac) Empires)</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Scramble for Africa</b>, also called the <b>Partition of Africa</b>, or <b>Conquest of Africa</b>,  was the invasion, annexation, division, and <a href="/wiki/Colonisation_of_Africa" title="Colonisation of Africa">colonization of most of Africa</a> by seven <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western European</a> powers during a short period known as <a href="/wiki/New_Imperialism" title="New Imperialism">New Imperialism</a> (between 1881 and 1914). The 10 percent of Africa that was under formal European control in 1870 increased to almost 90 percent by 1914, with only <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Ethiopia</a> (Abyssinia) and <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a> remaining independent, although Ethiopia would later be <a href="/wiki/Second_Italo-Ethiopian_War" title="Second Italo-Ethiopian War">invaded</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italian_Ethiopia" title="Italian Ethiopia">occupied</a> by <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy" title="Kingdom of Italy">Italy</a> for five years, from 1936 to 1941.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Conference" title="Berlin Conference">Berlin Conference</a> of 1884, which regulated European colonization and trade in Africa, is usually accepted as the beginning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> In the last quarter of the 19th century, there were considerable political rivalries within the empires of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_continent" title="European continent">European continent</a>, leading to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_continent" title="African continent">African continent</a> being partitioned without wars between European nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The later years of the 19th century saw a transition from "<a href="/wiki/Informal_empire" title="Informal empire">informal imperialism</a>" – military influence and economic dominance – to direct rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShillington2005301_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShillington2005301-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>By 1841, businessmen from Europe had established small trading posts along the coasts of Africa, but they seldom moved inland, preferring to stay near the sea. They primarily traded with locals. Large parts of the continent were essentially uninhabitable for Europeans because of their high mortality rates from <a href="/wiki/Tropical_disease" title="Tropical disease">tropical diseases</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> In the middle of the 19th century, European explorers mapped much of <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">Central Africa</a>.
</p><p>As late as the 1870s, Europeans controlled approximately 10% of the African continent, with all their territories located near the coasts. The most important holdings were <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Angola" title="Portuguese Angola">Angola</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Mozambique" title="Portuguese Mozambique">Mozambique</a>, held by Portugal; the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Colony" title="Cape Colony">Cape Colony</a>, held by Great Britain; and <a href="/wiki/French_Algeria" title="French Algeria">Algeria</a>, held by France. By 1914, only Ethiopia and Liberia remained independent of European control, with the latter having strong connections to the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Technological advances facilitated European expansion overseas. <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrialization</a> brought about rapid advancements in transportation and communication, especially in the forms of steamships, railways and telegraphs. Medical advances also played an important role, especially medicines for tropical diseases, which helped control their adverse effects. The development of <a href="/wiki/Quinine" title="Quinine">quinine</a>, an effective treatment for malaria, made vast expanses of the tropics more accessible for Europeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa_and_global_markets">Africa and global markets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Africa and global markets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:African-civilizations-map-pre-colonial.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1295" data-file-width="1390" decoding="async" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/African-civilizations-map-pre-colonial.svg/310px-African-civilizations-map-pre-colonial.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/African-civilizations-map-pre-colonial.svg/465px-African-civilizations-map-pre-colonial.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/African-civilizations-map-pre-colonial.svg/620px-African-civilizations-map-pre-colonial.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:African-civilizations-map-pre-colonial.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of African civilizations and kingdoms prior to European colonialism (spanning roughly 500 BCE to 1500 CE)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scramble-for-Africa-1880-1913-v2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1130" data-file-width="2280" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Scramble-for-Africa-1880-1913-v2.png/350px-Scramble-for-Africa-1880-1913-v2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Scramble-for-Africa-1880-1913-v2.png/525px-Scramble-for-Africa-1880-1913-v2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Scramble-for-Africa-1880-1913-v2.png/700px-Scramble-for-Africa-1880-1913-v2.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scramble-for-Africa-1880-1913-v2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of Africa in the years 1880 and 1913</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>, one of the last regions of the world largely untouched by "informal imperialism", was attractive to business entrepreneurs. During a time when Britain's <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_trade" title="Balance of trade">balance of trade</a> showed a growing deficit, with shrinking and increasingly <a href="/wiki/Protectionism" title="Protectionism">protectionist</a> continental markets during the <a href="/wiki/Long_Depression" title="Long Depression">Long Depression</a> (1873–96), Africa offered Britain, Germany, France, and other countries an open market that would garner them a trade surplus: a market that bought more from the colonial power than it sold overall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShillington2005301_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShillington2005301-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Surplus capital was often more profitably invested overseas, where cheap materials, limited competition, and abundant raw materials made a greater premium possible. Another inducement for imperialism arose from the demand for raw materials, especially <a href="/wiki/Ivory_trade" title="Ivory trade">ivory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_rubber" title="Natural rubber">rubber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">cocoa</a>, diamonds, tea, and tin. Additionally, Britain wanted control of areas of southern and eastern coasts of Africa for stopover ports on the route to Asia and its <a href="/wiki/Presidencies_and_provinces_of_British_India" title="Presidencies and provinces of British India">empire in India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> But, excluding the area which became the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">Union of South Africa</a> in 1910, European nations invested relatively limited amounts of capital in Africa compared to that in other continents. Consequently, the companies involved in tropical African commerce were relatively small, apart from <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Rhodes" title="Cecil Rhodes">Cecil Rhodes</a>'s <a href="/wiki/De_Beers" title="De Beers">De Beers Mining Company</a>. Rhodes had carved out <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a> for himself. <a href="/wiki/Leopold_II_of_Belgium" title="Leopold II of Belgium">Leopold II of Belgium</a> created the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Free_State" title="Congo Free State">Congo Free State</a> for rubber and other resource production.
</p><p>Pro-imperialist colonial lobbyists such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Pan-German_League" title="Pan-German League">Alldeutscher Verband</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Crispi" title="Francesco Crispi">Francesco Crispi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jules_Ferry" title="Jules Ferry">Jules Ferry</a>, argued that sheltered overseas markets in Africa would solve the problems of low prices and <a href="/wiki/Overproduction" title="Overproduction">overproduction</a> caused by shrinking continental markets. <a href="/wiki/J._A._Hobson" title="J. A. Hobson">John A. Hobson</a> argued in <i><a href="/wiki/Imperialism_(Hobson_book)" title="Imperialism (Hobson book)">Imperialism</a></i> that this shrinking of continental markets was a key factor of the global "New Imperialism" period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Easterly" title="William Easterly">William Easterly</a>, however, disagrees with the link made between <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a>, arguing that <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a> is used mostly to promote state-led development rather than corporate development. He has said that "imperialism is not so clearly linked to capitalism and the free markets... historically there has been a closer link between colonialism/imperialism and state-led approaches to development."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Strategic_rivalry">Strategic rivalry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Strategic rivalry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LeCommandantMarchand.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1408" data-file-width="974" decoding="async" height="318" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/LeCommandantMarchand.jpg/220px-LeCommandantMarchand.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/LeCommandantMarchand.jpg/330px-LeCommandantMarchand.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/LeCommandantMarchand.jpg/440px-LeCommandantMarchand.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LeCommandantMarchand.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Contemporary French propaganda poster hailing Major <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Marchand" title="Jean-Baptiste Marchand">Marchand</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Fashoda_Incident" title="Fashoda Incident">trek across Africa toward Fashoda</a> in 1898</div></div></div>
<p>While tropical Africa was not a large zone of investment, other overseas regions were. The vast interior between Egypt and the gold and diamond-rich <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a> had strategic value in securing the flow of overseas trade. Britain was under political pressure to build up lucrative markets in India, <a href="/wiki/British_Malaya" title="British Malaya">Malaya</a>, Australia and New Zealand. Thus, it wanted to secure the key waterway between East and West – the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a>, completed in 1869. However, a theory that Britain sought to annex East Africa during the 1880 onwards, out of geo-strategic concerns connected to Egypt (especially the Suez Canal),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> has been challenged by historians such as <a href="/wiki/John_Darwin_(historian)" title="John Darwin (historian)">John Darwin</a> (1997) and Jonas F. Gjersø (2015).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The scramble for African territory also reflected concern for the acquisition of military and naval bases, for strategic purposes and the exercise of power. The growing navies, and new ships driven by steam power, required coaling stations and ports for maintenance. Defence bases were also needed for the protection of sea routes and communication lines, particularly of expensive and vital international waterways such as the Suez Canal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1982_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1982-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Colonies were seen as assets in <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_power_(international_relations)" title="Balance of power (international relations)">balance of power</a> negotiations, useful as items of exchange at times of international bargaining. Colonies with large native populations were also a source of military power; Britain and France used large numbers of <a href="/wiki/Sepoy#Sepoys_in_British_India" title="Sepoy">British Indian</a> and North African soldiers, respectively, in many of their colonial wars (and would do so again in the coming World Wars). In the age of <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a> there was pressure for a nation to acquire an empire as a status symbol; the idea of "greatness" became linked with the "<a href="/wiki/The_White_Man%27s_Burden" title="The White Man's Burden">White Man's Burden</a>", or sense of duty, underlying many nations' strategies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1982_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1982-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>
In the early 1880s, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Savorgnan_de_Brazza" title="Pierre Savorgnan de Brazza">Pierre Savorgnan de Brazza</a> was exploring the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kongo" title="Kingdom of Kongo">Kingdom of Kongo</a> for France, at the same time <a href="/wiki/Henry_Morton_Stanley" title="Henry Morton Stanley">Henry Morton Stanley</a> explored it on behalf of <a href="/wiki/Leopold_II_of_Belgium" title="Leopold II of Belgium">Leopold II of Belgium</a>, who would have it as his personal <a href="/wiki/Congo_Free_State" title="Congo Free State">Congo Free State</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-17">[17]</a></sup> France occupied Tunisia in May 1881, which may have convinced Italy to join the German-Austrian <a href="/wiki/Dual_Alliance_(1879)" title="Dual Alliance (1879)">Dual Alliance</a> in 1882, thus forming the <a href="/wiki/Triple_Alliance_(1882)" title="Triple Alliance (1882)">Triple Alliance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The same year, Britain occupied Egypt (hitherto an autonomous state owing nominal <a href="/wiki/Fealty" title="Fealty">fealty</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>), which ruled over Sudan and parts of Chad, Eritrea, and Somalia. In 1884, Germany declared <a href="/wiki/Togoland" title="Togoland">Togoland</a>, the Cameroons and <a href="/wiki/South_West_Africa" title="South West Africa">South West Africa</a> to be under its protection;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> and France occupied Guinea. <a href="/wiki/French_West_Africa" title="French West Africa">French West Africa</a> was founded in 1895 and <a href="/wiki/French_Equatorial_Africa" title="French Equatorial Africa">French Equatorial Africa</a> in 1910.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/French_Somaliland" title="French Somaliland">French Somaliland</a>, a short-lived <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian</a> colony in the <a href="/wiki/Khedivate_of_Egypt" title="Khedivate of Egypt">Egyptian</a> fort of <a href="/wiki/Sagallo" title="Sagallo">Sagallo</a> was briefly proclaimed by <a href="/wiki/Terek_Cossacks" title="Terek Cossacks">Terek Cossacks</a> in 1889.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Preaching_from_a_Waggon_(David_Livingstone)_by_The_London_Missionary_Society.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Preaching_from_a_Waggon_%28David_Livingstone%29_by_The_London_Missionary_Society.jpg/220px-Preaching_from_a_Waggon_%28David_Livingstone%29_by_The_London_Missionary_Society.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Preaching_from_a_Waggon_%28David_Livingstone%29_by_The_London_Missionary_Society.jpg/330px-Preaching_from_a_Waggon_%28David_Livingstone%29_by_The_London_Missionary_Society.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Preaching_from_a_Waggon_%28David_Livingstone%29_by_The_London_Missionary_Society.jpg/440px-Preaching_from_a_Waggon_%28David_Livingstone%29_by_The_London_Missionary_Society.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Preaching_from_a_Waggon_(David_Livingstone)_by_The_London_Missionary_Society.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/David_Livingstone" title="David Livingstone">David Livingstone</a>, early explorer of the interior of Africa and fighter against the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_slave_trade" title="African slave trade">slave trade</a></div></div></div>
<h4><span id="Germany.27s_Weltpolitik"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany's_Weltpolitik">Germany's Weltpolitik</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Germany's Weltpolitik">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA6364,_Deutsch-Ostafrika,_Polizeiaskaris.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="532" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA6364%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Polizeiaskaris.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA6364%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Polizeiaskaris.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA6364%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Polizeiaskaris.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA6364%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Polizeiaskaris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA6364%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Polizeiaskaris.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA6364%2C_Deutsch-Ostafrika%2C_Polizeiaskaris.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_105-DOA6364,_Deutsch-Ostafrika,_Polizeiaskaris.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Askari" title="Askari">Askari</a> colonial troops in <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a>, c. 1906</div></div></div>
<p>Germany, divided into small states, was not a colonial power before it unified in 1871. Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a> disliked colonies but gave in to popular and elite pressure in the 1880s. He sponsored the 1884–85 <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Conference" title="Berlin Conference">Berlin Conference</a>, which set the rules of effective control of African territories, and reduced the risk of conflict between colonial powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Bismarck used private companies to set up small colonial operations in Africa and the Pacific.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pan-Germanism" title="Pan-Germanism">Pan-Germanism</a> became linked to the young nation's new imperialist drives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> In the beginning of the 1880s, the <i>Deutscher Kolonialverein</i> was created, and published the <i>Kolonialzeitung</i>. This colonial lobby was also relayed by the nationalist <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alldeutscher_Verband" title="Alldeutscher Verband">Alldeutscher Verband</a></i>. <i><a href="/wiki/Weltpolitik" title="Weltpolitik">Weltpolitik</a></i> (world policy) was the foreign policy adopted by Kaiser <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II,_German_Emperor" title="Wilhelm II, German Emperor">Wilhelm II</a> in 1890, with the aim of transforming Germany into a global power through aggressive diplomacy, and the development of a large navy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Germany became the third-largest colonial power in Africa, the location of most of its 2.6 million square kilometres of colonial territory and 14 million colonial subjects in 1914. The African possessions were Southwest Africa, Togoland, the Cameroons, and Tanganyika.  Germany tried to isolate France in 1905 with the <a href="/wiki/First_Moroccan_Crisis" title="First Moroccan Crisis">First Moroccan Crisis</a>. This led to the 1905 <a href="/wiki/Algeciras_Conference" title="Algeciras Conference">Algeciras Conference</a>, in which France's influence on Morocco was compensated by the exchange of other territories, and then to the <a href="/wiki/Agadir_Crisis" title="Agadir Crisis">Agadir Crisis</a> in 1911.
</p>
<h4><span id="Italy.27s_expansion"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Italy's_expansion">Italy's expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Italy's expansion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Italian_aircraft_attacking_Ottoman_forces_in_Libya_1911_or_1912.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="317" data-file-width="236" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Italian_aircraft_attacking_Ottoman_forces_in_Libya_1911_or_1912.jpg/170px-Italian_aircraft_attacking_Ottoman_forces_in_Libya_1911_or_1912.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e1/Italian_aircraft_attacking_Ottoman_forces_in_Libya_1911_or_1912.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Italian_aircraft_attacking_Ottoman_forces_in_Libya_1911_or_1912.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Italian aircraft in action against Ottoman forces during the <a href="/wiki/Italian_invasion_of_Libya" title="Italian invasion of Libya">Italian invasion of Libya</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Italo-Turkish_War" title="Italo-Turkish War">Italo-Turkish War</a></div></div></div>
<p>After a <a href="/wiki/Second_Italian_War_of_Independence" title="Second Italian War of Independence">war with Austria in 1859</a>, Italy was largely unified into the Kingdom of Italy in 1861. Italy sought to expand its territory and become a great power, <a href="/wiki/Italian_Eritrea" title="Italian Eritrea">taking possession</a> of parts of <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a> in 1870<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> and 1882. In 1889–90, it occupied territory on the south side of the horn of Africa, forming what would become <a href="/wiki/Italian_Somaliland" title="Italian Somaliland">Italian Somaliland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In the disorder that followed the 1889 death of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Yohannes_IV" title="Yohannes IV">Yohannes IV</a>, General <a href="/wiki/Oreste_Baratieri" title="Oreste Baratieri">Oreste Baratieri</a> occupied the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Highlands" title="Ethiopian Highlands">Ethiopian Highlands</a> along the Eritrean coast, and Italy proclaimed the establishment of a new colony of Eritrea, with its capital moved from <a href="/wiki/Massawa" title="Massawa">Massawa</a> to <a href="/wiki/Asmara" title="Asmara">Asmara</a>. When relations between Italy and Ethiopia deteriorated, the <a href="/wiki/First_Italo-Ethiopian_War" title="First Italo-Ethiopian War">First Italo-Ethiopian War</a> broke out in 1895; Italian troops were defeated as the Ethiopians had numerical superiority, better organization, and support from Russia and France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In 1911, Italy engaged in a <a href="/wiki/Italo-Turkish_War" title="Italo-Turkish War">war with the Ottoman Empire</a>, in which it acquired <a href="/wiki/Italian_Tripolitania" title="Italian Tripolitania">Tripolitania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italian_Cyrenaica" title="Italian Cyrenaica">Cyrenaica</a>, that together formed what became known as <a href="/wiki/Italian_Libya" title="Italian Libya">Italian Libya</a>. In 1919 <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Corradini" title="Enrico Corradini">Enrico Corradini</a> developed the concept of <i>Proletarian Nationalism</i>, which was supposed to legitimise Italy's imperialism by a mixture of socialism with nationalism:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>We must start by recognizing the fact that there are proletarian nations as well as proletarian classes; that is to say, there are nations whose living conditions are subject...to the way of life of other nations, just as classes are. Once this is realised, nationalism must insist firmly on this truth: Italy is, materially and morally, a proletarian nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Italo-Abyssinian_War" title="Second Italo-Abyssinian War">Second Italo-Abyssinian War</a> (1935–36), ordered by the <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">fascist</a> dictator <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a>, was the last colonial war (that is, intended to colonise a country, as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_national_liberation" title="Wars of national liberation">wars of national liberation</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> occupying <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>—which had remained the last independent African territory, apart from Liberia. <a href="/wiki/Italian_Ethiopia" title="Italian Ethiopia">Italian Ethiopia</a> was occupied by fascist Italian forces in World War II as part of <a href="/wiki/Italian_East_Africa" title="Italian East Africa">Italian East Africa</a> though much of the mountainous countryside had remained out Italian control due to resistance from the <a href="/wiki/Arbegnoch" title="Arbegnoch">Arbegnoch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The occupation is an example of the expansionist policy that characterized the <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis powers</a> as opposed to the Scramble for Africa.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonization_prior_to_World_War_I">Colonization prior to World War I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Colonization prior to World War I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Congo">Congo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Congo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Morton_Stanley.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="266" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Henry_Morton_Stanley.jpg/170px-Henry_Morton_Stanley.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Henry_Morton_Stanley.jpg/255px-Henry_Morton_Stanley.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/36/Henry_Morton_Stanley.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Morton_Stanley.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henry_Morton_Stanley" title="Henry Morton Stanley">Henry Morton Stanley</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/David_Livingstone" title="David Livingstone">David Livingstone</a>'s explorations, carried on by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Morton_Stanley" title="Henry Morton Stanley">Henry Morton Stanley</a>, excited imaginations with Stanley's grandiose ideas for colonisation; but these found little support owing to the problems and scale of action required, except from Leopold II of Belgium, who in 1876 had organised the <a href="/wiki/International_African_Association" title="International African Association">International African Association</a>. From 1869 to 1874, Stanley was secretly sent by Leopold II to the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Basin" title="Congo Basin">Congo</a> region, where he made treaties with several African chiefs along the <a href="/wiki/Congo_River" title="Congo River">Congo River</a> and by 1882 had sufficient territory to form the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Free_State" title="Congo Free State">Congo Free State</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pierre_Savorgnan_de_Brazza.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="273" data-file-width="200" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Pierre_Savorgnan_de_Brazza.jpg/170px-Pierre_Savorgnan_de_Brazza.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cb/Pierre_Savorgnan_de_Brazza.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pierre_Savorgnan_de_Brazza.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pierre_Savorgnan_de_Brazza" title="Pierre Savorgnan de Brazza">Pierre Savorgnan de Brazza</a> in his version of "native" dress, photographed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/F%C3%A9lix_Nadar" title="Félix Nadar">Félix Nadar</a></div></div></div>
<p>While <a href="/wiki/Henry_Morton_Stanley%27s_first_trans-Africa_expedition" title="Henry Morton Stanley's first trans-Africa expedition">Stanley was exploring the Congo</a> on behalf of Leopold II of Belgium, the Franco-Italian marine officer Pierre de Brazza travelled into the western Congo Basin and raised the French flag over the newly founded <a href="/wiki/Brazzaville" title="Brazzaville">Brazzaville</a> in 1881, thus occupying today's <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Republic of the Congo">Republic of the Congo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-17">[17]</a></sup> Portugal, which also claimed the area because of old treaties with the Kingdom of Kongo, made a treaty with Britain on 26 February 1884 to block off Leopold's access to the Atlantic.
</p><p>By 1890 the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Free_State" title="Congo Free State">Congo Free State</a> had consolidated control of its territory between <a href="/wiki/Kinshasa" title="Kinshasa">Leopoldville</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kisangani" title="Kisangani">Stanleyville</a> and was looking to push south down the <a href="/wiki/Lualaba_River" title="Lualaba River">Lualaba River</a> from Stanleyville. At the same time, the <a href="/wiki/British_South_Africa_Company" title="British South Africa Company">British South Africa Company</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Rhodes" title="Cecil Rhodes">Cecil Rhodes</a> was expanding north from the <a href="/wiki/Limpopo_River" title="Limpopo River">Limpopo River</a>, sending the <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_Column" title="Pioneer Column">Pioneer Column</a> (guided by <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Selous" title="Frederick Selous">Frederick Selous</a>) through <a href="/wiki/Matabeleland" title="Matabeleland">Matabeleland</a>, and starting a colony in <a href="/wiki/Mashonaland" title="Mashonaland">Mashonaland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tippu_Tip" title="Tippu Tip">Tippu Tip</a>, a Zanzibari Arab based in the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Zanzibar" title="Sultanate of Zanzibar">Sultanate of Zanzibar</a>, also played a major role as a "protector of European explorers", ivory trader and slave trader. Having established a trading empire within Zanzibar and neighboring areas in East Africa, Tippu Tip would shift his alignment towards the rising colonial powers in the region and at the proposal of Henry Morton Stanley, Tippu Tip became a governor of the "<a href="/wiki/Stanley_Falls_District" title="Stanley Falls District">Stanley Falls District</a>" (<a href="/wiki/Boyoma_Falls" title="Boyoma Falls">Boyoma Falls</a>) in Leopold's Congo Free State, before being involved in the <a href="/wiki/Congo%E2%80%93Arab_War" title="Congo–Arab War">Congo–Arab War</a> against Leopold II's colonial state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>To the west, in the land where their expansions would meet, was <a href="/wiki/Katanga_Province" title="Katanga Province">Katanga</a>, site of the <a href="/wiki/Yeke_Kingdom" title="Yeke Kingdom">Yeke Kingdom</a> of <a href="/wiki/Msiri" title="Msiri">Msiri</a>. Msiri was the most militarily powerful ruler in the area and traded large quantities of copper, ivory and slaves—and rumors of gold reached European ears.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The scramble for Katanga was a prime example of the period. Rhodes sent two expeditions to Msiri in 1890 led by <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Sharpe" title="Alfred Sharpe">Alfred Sharpe</a>, who was rebuffed, and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Thomson_(explorer)" title="Joseph Thomson (explorer)">Joseph Thomson</a>, who failed to reach Katanga. Leopold sent four expeditions. First, the <a href="/wiki/Paul_Le_Marinel" title="Paul Le Marinel">Le Marinel expedition</a> could only extract a vaguely worded letter. The <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Delcommune" title="Alexandre Delcommune">Delcommune expedition</a> was rebuffed. The well-armed <a href="/wiki/Stairs_Expedition_to_Katanga" title="Stairs Expedition to Katanga">Stairs expedition</a> was given orders to take Katanga with or without Msiri's consent. Msiri refused, was shot, and his head was cut off and stuck it on a pole as a "barbaric lesson" to the people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bia_River" title="Bia River">Bia River</a> expedition finished the job of establishing an administration of sorts and a "police presence" in Katanga. Thus, the half million square kilometres of Katanga came into Leopold's possession and brought his African realm up to 2,300,000 square kilometres (890,000 sq mi), about 75 times larger than Belgium. The Congo Free State imposed such a <a href="/wiki/State_terrorism" title="State terrorism">terror regime</a> on the colonized people, including mass killings and forced labour, that Belgium, under pressure from the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Reform_Association" title="Congo Reform Association">Congo Reform Association</a>, ended Leopold II's rule and annexed it on 20 August 1908 as a colony of Belgium, known as the <a href="/wiki/Belgian_Congo" title="Belgian Congo">Belgian Congo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MutilatedChildrenFromCongo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="969" data-file-width="646" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/MutilatedChildrenFromCongo.jpg/220px-MutilatedChildrenFromCongo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/MutilatedChildrenFromCongo.jpg/330px-MutilatedChildrenFromCongo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/MutilatedChildrenFromCongo.jpg/440px-MutilatedChildrenFromCongo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MutilatedChildrenFromCongo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>From 1885 to 1908, <a href="/wiki/Atrocities_in_the_Congo_Free_State" title="Atrocities in the Congo Free State">many atrocities</a> were perpetrated in the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Free_State" title="Congo Free State">Congo Free State</a>; in the image Native Congo Free State labourers who failed to meet rubber collection quotas punished by having their hands cut off.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Atrocities_in_the_Congo_Free_State" title="Atrocities in the Congo Free State">brutality of King Leopold II</a> in his former colony of the Congo Free State<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> was well documented; up to 8 million of the estimated 16 million native inhabitants died between 1885 and 1908.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_30.08.2012_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_30.08.2012-41">[41]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Roger_Casement" title="Roger Casement">Roger Casement</a>, an Irish diplomat of the time, this depopulation had four main causes: "indiscriminate war", starvation, reduction of births and diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHochschild2006226–32_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHochschild2006226–32-42">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/African_trypanosomiasis" title="African trypanosomiasis">Sleeping sickness</a> ravaged the country and must also be taken into account for the dramatic decrease in population; it has been estimated that sleeping sickness and <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> killed nearly half the population in the areas surrounding the lower Congo River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Estimates of the death toll vary considerably. As the first census did not take place until 1924, it is difficult to quantify the population loss of the period. The <a href="/wiki/Casement_Report" title="Casement Report">Casement Report</a> set it at three million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Rubinstein" title="William Rubinstein">William Rubinstein</a> writes: "More basically, it appears almost certain that the population figures given by <a href="/wiki/Adam_Hochschild" title="Adam Hochschild">Hochschild</a> are inaccurate. There is, of course, no way of ascertaining the population of the Congo before the twentieth century, and estimates like 20 million are purely guesses. Most of the interior of the Congo was literally unexplored if not inaccessible."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>A similar situation occurred in the neighbouring <a href="/wiki/French_Congo" title="French Congo">French Congo</a>, where most of the resource extraction was run by concession companies, whose brutal methods, along with the introduction of disease, resulted in the loss of up to 50% of the indigenous population according to Hochschild.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The French government appointed a commission headed by de Brazza in 1905 to investigate the rumoured abuses in the colony. However, de Brazza died on the return trip, and his "searingly critical" report was neither acted upon nor released to the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHochschild2006280–81_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHochschild2006280–81-47">[47]</a></sup> In the 1920s, about 20,000 forced labourers died building a railroad through the French territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Egypt.2C_Sudan.2C_and_South_Sudan"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Egypt,_Sudan,_and_South_Sudan">Egypt, Sudan, and South Sudan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Egypt, Sudan, and South Sudan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Suez_Canal">Suez Canal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Suez Canal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Port_Said_Suez_Canal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="421" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Port_Said_Suez_Canal.jpg/220px-Port_Said_Suez_Canal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Port_Said_Suez_Canal.jpg/330px-Port_Said_Suez_Canal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Port_Said_Suez_Canal.jpg/440px-Port_Said_Suez_Canal.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Port_Said_Suez_Canal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Port_Said" title="Port Said">Port Said</a> entrance to Suez Canal, showing De Lesseps' statue</div></div></div>
<p>In order to construct the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a>, French diplomat <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_de_Lesseps" title="Ferdinand de Lesseps">Ferdinand de Lesseps</a> had obtained many concessions from <a href="/wiki/Isma%27il_Pasha" title="Isma'il Pasha">Isma'il Pasha</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Khedive" title="Khedive">Khedive</a> of Egypt and Sudan in 1854–56. Some sources estimate the workforce at 30,000,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> but others estimate that 120,000 workers died over the ten years of construction from malnutrition, fatigue, and disease, especially <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Shortly before its completion in 1869, Khedive Isma'il borrowed enormous sums from British and French bankers at high rates of interest. By 1875, he was facing financial difficulties and was forced to sell his block of shares in the Suez Canal. The shares were snapped up by Britain, under Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Benjamin Disraeli</a>, who sought to give his country practical control in the management of this strategic waterway. When Isma'il repudiated Egypt's foreign debt in 1879, Britain and France seized joint financial control over the country, forcing the Egyptian ruler to abdicate and installing his eldest son <a href="/wiki/Tewfik_Pasha" title="Tewfik Pasha">Tewfik Pasha</a> in his place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The Egyptian and Sudanese ruling classes did not relish foreign intervention.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mahdist_War">Mahdist War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Mahdist War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>During the 1870s, European initiatives against the slave trade caused an economic crisis in northern Sudan, precipitating the rise of <a href="/wiki/Mahdist_State" title="Mahdist State">Mahdist</a> forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> In 1881, the <a href="/wiki/Mahdist_War" title="Mahdist War">Mahdist revolt</a> erupted in Sudan under <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ahmad" title="Muhammad Ahmad">Muhammad Ahmad</a>, severing Tewfik's authority in Sudan. The same year, Tewfik suffered an even more perilous rebellion by his own Egyptian army in the form of the <a href="/wiki/%CA%BBUrabi_revolt" title="ʻUrabi revolt">Urabi revolt</a>. In 1882, Tewfik appealed for direct British military assistance, commencing Britain's administration of Egypt. A joint British-Egyptian military force entered in the Mahdist War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-53">[53]</a></sup> Additionally the Egyptian province of <a href="/wiki/Equatoria" title="Equatoria">Equatoria</a> (located in South Sudan) led by <a href="/wiki/Emin_Pasha" title="Emin Pasha">Emin Pasha</a> was also subject to an ostensible <a href="/wiki/Emin_Pasha_Relief_Expedition" title="Emin Pasha Relief Expedition">relief expedition of Emin Pasha</a> against Mahdist forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The British-Egyptian force ultimately defeated the Mahdist forces in Sudan in 1898.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-53">[53]</a></sup> Thereafter, Britain seized effective control of Sudan, which was nominally called <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan" title="Anglo-Egyptian Sudan">Anglo-Egyptian Sudan</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Berlin_Conference_.281884.E2.80.931885.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Berlin_Conference_(1884–1885)">Berlin Conference (1884–1885)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Berlin Conference (1884–1885)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Conference" title="Berlin Conference">Berlin Conference</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kongokonferenz.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2790" data-file-width="3631" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Kongokonferenz.jpg/220px-Kongokonferenz.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Kongokonferenz.jpg/330px-Kongokonferenz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Kongokonferenz.jpg/440px-Kongokonferenz.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kongokonferenz.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Otto von Bismarck at the Berlin Conference, 1884</div></div></div>
<p>The occupation of Egypt and the acquisition of the Congo were the first major moves in what came to be a precipitous scramble for African territory. In 1884, <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Bismarck" title="Otto von Bismarck">Otto von Bismarck</a> convened the 1884–1885 Berlin Conference to discuss the African problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> While diplomatic discussions were held regarding ending the remaining slave trade as well as the reach of missionary activities, the primary concern of those in attendance was preventing war between the European powers as they divided the continent among themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> More importantly, the diplomats in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> laid down the rules of competition by which the great powers were to be guided in seeking colonies. They also agreed that the area along the Congo River was to be administered by Leopold II as a neutral area in which trade and navigation were to be free.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> No nation was to stake claims in Africa without notifying other powers of its intentions. No territory could be formally claimed prior to being effectively occupied. However, the competitors ignored the rules when convenient, and on several occasions war was only narrowly avoided (see <a href="/wiki/Fashoda_Incident" title="Fashoda Incident">Fashoda Incident</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Swahili_coast" title="Swahili coast">Swahili coast</a> territories of the Sultanate of Zanzibar were partitioned between Germany and Britain, initially leaving the archipelago of <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> independent until 1890, when that remnant of the Sultanate was made into a British protectorate with the <a href="/wiki/Heligoland%E2%80%93Zanzibar_Treaty" title="Heligoland–Zanzibar Treaty">Heligoland–Zanzibar Treaty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Britain.27s_administration_of_Egypt_and_South_Africa"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Britain's_administration_of_Egypt_and_South_Africa">Britain's administration of Egypt and South Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Britain's administration of Egypt and South Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LizzieVanZyl.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1644" data-file-width="2412" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/LizzieVanZyl.jpg/220px-LizzieVanZyl.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/LizzieVanZyl.jpg/330px-LizzieVanZyl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/LizzieVanZyl.jpg/440px-LizzieVanZyl.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LizzieVanZyl.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boer" title="Boer">Boer</a> child in a <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War_concentration_camps" title="Second Boer War concentration camps">British concentration camp</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a> (1899–1902)</div></div></div>
<p>Britain's administration of Egypt and the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Colony" title="Cape Colony">Cape Colony</a> contributed to a preoccupation over securing the source of the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Egypt was taken over by the British in 1882, leaving the Ottoman Empire in a nominal role until 1914, when London made it a protectorate. Egypt was never an actual British colony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Sudan, Nigeria, Kenya, and Uganda were subjugated in the 1890s and early 20th century; and in the south, the Cape Colony (first acquired in 1795) provided a base for the subjugation of neighbouring African states and the Dutch <a href="/wiki/Afrikaners" title="Afrikaners">Afrikaner</a> settlers who had left the Cape to avoid the British and then founded their own republics. <a href="/wiki/Theophilus_Shepstone" title="Theophilus Shepstone">Theophilus Shepstone</a> annexed the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Republic" title="South African Republic">South African Republic</a> in 1877 for the British Empire, after it had been independent for twenty years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> In 1879, after the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Zulu_War" title="Anglo-Zulu War">Anglo-Zulu War</a>, Britain consolidated its control of most of the territories of South Africa. The Boers protested, and in December 1880 they revolted, leading to the <a href="/wiki/First_Boer_War" title="First Boer War">First Boer War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Gladstone</a> signed a peace treaty on 23 March 1881, giving self-government to the <a href="/wiki/Boers" title="Boers">Boers</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Transvaal_Colony" title="Transvaal Colony">Transvaal</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Jameson_Raid" title="Jameson Raid">Jameson Raid</a> of 1895 was a failed attempt by the British South Africa Company and the <a href="/wiki/Johannesburg_Reform_Committee" title="Johannesburg Reform Committee">Johannesburg Reform Committee</a> to overthrow the Boer government in the Transvaal. The <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a>, fought between 1899 and 1902, was about control of the gold and diamond industries; the independent Boer republics of the <a href="/wiki/Orange_Free_State" title="Orange Free State">Orange Free State</a> and the South African Republic were this time defeated and absorbed into the British Empire.
</p><p>The French thrust into the African interior was mainly from the coasts of <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a> (present-day Senegal) eastward, through the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> along the southern border of the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a>. Their ultimate aim was to have an uninterrupted colonial empire from the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger River</a> to the Nile, thus controlling all trade to and from the Sahel region by virtue of their existing control over the caravan routes through the Sahara. The British, on the other hand, wanted to link their possessions in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a> with their territories in <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> and these two areas with the Nile basin.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Ahmad.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="928" data-file-width="724" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Muhammad_Ahmad.jpg/220px-Muhammad_Ahmad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Muhammad_Ahmad.jpg/330px-Muhammad_Ahmad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Muhammad_Ahmad.jpg/440px-Muhammad_Ahmad.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Muhammad_Ahmad.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ahmad" title="Muhammad Ahmad">Muhammad Ahmad</a>, leader of the Mahdists. This fundamentalist group of Muslim dervishes overran much of <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mahdist_War" title="Mahdist War">fought</a> British forces.</div></div></div>
<p>The Sudan (which included most of present-day Uganda) was the key to the fulfillment of these ambitions, especially since Egypt was already under British control. This "red line" through Africa is made most famous by Cecil Rhodes. Along with <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Milner,_1st_Viscount_Milner" title="Alfred Milner, 1st Viscount Milner">Lord Milner</a>, the British colonial minister in South Africa, Rhodes advocated such a "Cape to Cairo" empire, linking the Suez Canal to the mineral-rich South Africa by rail. Though hampered by German occupation of <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">Tanganyika</a> until the end of World War I, Rhodes successfully lobbied on behalf of such a sprawling African empire.
</p><p>If one draws a line from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a> to <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a> (Rhodes's dream), and one from <a href="/wiki/Dakar" title="Dakar">Dakar</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> (the French ambition), these two lines intersect somewhere in eastern Sudan near <a href="/wiki/Fashoda_State" title="Fashoda State">Fashoda</a>, explaining its strategic importance. In short, Britain had sought to extend its East African empire contiguously from Cairo to the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a>, while France had sought to extend its own holdings from Dakar to the Sudan, which would enable its empire to span the entire continent from the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>.
</p><p>A French force under <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Marchand" title="Jean-Baptiste Marchand">Jean-Baptiste Marchand</a> arrived first at the strategically located fort at Fashoda, soon followed by a British force under <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Kitchener,_1st_Earl_Kitchener" title="Herbert Kitchener, 1st Earl Kitchener">Lord Kitchener</a>, commander in chief of the British Army since 1892. The French withdrew after a standoff and continued to press claims to other posts in the region. The <a href="/wiki/Fashoda_Incident" title="Fashoda Incident">Fashoda Incident</a> ultimately led to the signature of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entente_cordiale" title="Entente cordiale">Entente Cordiale</a></i> of 1904, which guaranteed peace between the two.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moroccan_Crisis">Moroccan Crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Moroccan Crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/First_Moroccan_Crisis" title="First Moroccan Crisis">First Moroccan Crisis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agadir_Crisis" title="Agadir Crisis">Agadir Crisis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pacification.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="777" data-file-width="820" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Pacification.jpg/220px-Pacification.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Pacification.jpg/330px-Pacification.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Pacification.jpg/440px-Pacification.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pacification.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map depicting the staged <a href="/wiki/French_conquest_of_Morocco" title="French conquest of Morocco">pacification of Morocco</a> through to 1934</div></div></div><p>
Although the Berlin Conference had set the rules for the Scramble for Africa, it had not weakened the rival imperialists.  As a result of the <i>Entente Cordiale</i>, the German Kaiser decided to test the solidity of such influence, using the contested territory of <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> as a battlefield. Kaiser Wilhelm II visited <a href="/wiki/Tangier" title="Tangier">Tangier</a> on 31 March 1905 and made a speech in favour of Moroccan independence, challenging French influence in Morocco. France's presence had been reaffirmed by Britain and Spain in 1904. The Kaiser's speech bolstered French nationalism, and with British support the French foreign minister, <a href="/wiki/Th%C3%A9ophile_Delcass%C3%A9" title="Théophile Delcassé">Théophile Delcassé</a>, took a defiant line. The crisis peaked in mid-June 1905, when Delcassé was forced out of the ministry by the more conciliation-minded premier <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Rouvier" title="Maurice Rouvier">Maurice Rouvier</a>. But by July 1905 Germany was becoming isolated, and the French agreed to a conference to solve the crisis.</p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Si_Kaddour_ben_Ghabrit_et_le_sultan_Moufay_Hafid.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="957" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Si_Kaddour_ben_Ghabrit_et_le_sultan_Moufay_Hafid.jpg/220px-Si_Kaddour_ben_Ghabrit_et_le_sultan_Moufay_Hafid.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Si_Kaddour_ben_Ghabrit_et_le_sultan_Moufay_Hafid.jpg/330px-Si_Kaddour_ben_Ghabrit_et_le_sultan_Moufay_Hafid.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Si_Kaddour_ben_Ghabrit_et_le_sultan_Moufay_Hafid.jpg/440px-Si_Kaddour_ben_Ghabrit_et_le_sultan_Moufay_Hafid.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Si_Kaddour_ben_Ghabrit_et_le_sultan_Moufay_Hafid.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Moroccan <a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">Sultan</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdelhafid_of_Morocco" title="Abdelhafid of Morocco">Abdelhafid</a>, who led the resistance to French expansionism during the <a href="/wiki/Agadir_Crisis" title="Agadir Crisis">Agadir Crisis</a></div></div></div>
<p>The 1906 <a href="/wiki/Algeciras_Conference" title="Algeciras Conference">Algeciras Conference</a> was called to settle the dispute. Of the thirteen nations present, the German representatives found their only supporter was <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a>, which had no interest in Africa. France had firm support from Britain, the U.S., Russia, Italy and Spain. The Germans eventually accepted an agreement, signed on 31 May 1906, whereby France yielded certain domestic changes in Morocco but retained control of key areas.
</p><p>However, five years later the Second Moroccan Crisis (or <a href="/wiki/Agadir_Crisis" title="Agadir Crisis">Agadir Crisis</a>) was sparked by the deployment of the German gunboat <i><a href="/wiki/SMS_Panther" title="SMS Panther">Panther</a></i> to the port of <a href="/wiki/Agadir" title="Agadir">Agadir</a> in July 1911. Germany had started to attempt to match Britain's <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Royal_Navy_(after_1707)" title="History of the Royal Navy (after 1707)">naval supremacy</a>—the British navy had a policy of remaining larger than the next two rival fleets in the world combined. When the British heard of the <i>Panther</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s arrival in Morocco, they wrongly believed that the Germans meant to turn Agadir into a naval base on the Atlantic. The German move was aimed at reinforcing claims for compensation for acceptance of effective French control of the <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North African</a> kingdom, where France's pre-eminence had been upheld by the 1906 Algeciras Conference. In November 1911 a compromise was reached under which Germany accepted France's position in Morocco in return for a slice of territory in the <a href="/wiki/French_Equatorial_Africa" title="French Equatorial Africa">French Equatorial African</a> colony of <a href="/wiki/French_Congo" title="French Congo">Middle Congo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/French_protectorate_in_Morocco" title="French protectorate in Morocco">France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_protectorate_in_Morocco" title="Spanish protectorate in Morocco">Spain</a> subsequently established a full <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">protectorate</a> over Morocco on 30 March 1912, ending what remained of the country's formal independence. Furthermore, British backing for France during the two Moroccan crises reinforced the <i>Entente</i> between the two countries and added to Anglo-German estrangement, deepening the divisions that would culminate in the First World War.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dervish_resistance">Dervish resistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Dervish resistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the Berlin Conference, the British, Italians, and Ethiopians sought to claim lands inhabited by the Somalis. The <a href="/wiki/Dervish_movement_(Somali)" title="Dervish movement (Somali)">Dervish movement</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Abdullah_Hassan" title="Mohammed Abdullah Hassan">Sayid Muhammed Abdullah Hassan</a>, existed for 21 years, from 1899 until 1920. The Dervish movement successfully repulsed the British Empire four times and forced it to retreat to the coastal region. Because of these successful expeditions, the Dervish movement was recognized as an ally by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> and <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German</a> empires. The <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turks</a> named Hassan <a href="/wiki/Emir" title="Emir">Emir</a> of the Somali nation, and the Germans promised to officially recognise any territories the Dervishes were to acquire. After a quarter of a century of holding the British at bay, the <a href="/wiki/Somaliland_campaign_(1920)" title="Somaliland campaign (1920)">Dervishes were finally defeated in 1920</a> as a direct consequence of Britain's use of aircraft.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Herero_Wars_and_the_Maji_Maji_Rebellion">Herero Wars and the Maji Maji Rebellion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Herero Wars and the Maji Maji Rebellion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Herero_Wars" title="Herero Wars">Herero Wars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maji_Maji_Rebellion" title="Maji Maji Rebellion">Maji Maji Rebellion</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Herero_and_Namaqua_genocide" title="Herero and Namaqua genocide">Herero and Namaqua genocide</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herero_Nama_Shark_Island_Death_Camp_Lieutenant_von_Durling_05.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="317" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Herero_Nama_Shark_Island_Death_Camp_Lieutenant_von_Durling_05.jpg/170px-Herero_Nama_Shark_Island_Death_Camp_Lieutenant_von_Durling_05.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Herero_Nama_Shark_Island_Death_Camp_Lieutenant_von_Durling_05.jpg/255px-Herero_Nama_Shark_Island_Death_Camp_Lieutenant_von_Durling_05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5d/Herero_Nama_Shark_Island_Death_Camp_Lieutenant_von_Durling_05.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herero_Nama_Shark_Island_Death_Camp_Lieutenant_von_Durling_05.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lieutenant von Durling with prisoners at <a href="/wiki/Shark_Island_concentration_camp" title="Shark Island concentration camp">Shark Island</a>, one of the German concentration camps used during the <a href="/wiki/Herero_and_Namaqua_genocide" title="Herero and Namaqua genocide">Herero and Namaqua genocide</a></div></div></div>
<p>Between 1904 and 1908, Germany's colonies in <a href="/wiki/German_South_West_Africa" title="German South West Africa">German South West Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a> were rocked by separate, contemporaneous native revolts against their rule. In both territories the threat to German rule was quickly defeated once large-scale reinforcements from Germany arrived, with the <a href="/wiki/Herero_people" title="Herero people">Herero</a> rebels in German South West Africa being defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Waterberg" title="Battle of Waterberg">Battle of Waterberg</a> and the Maji-Maji rebels in German East Africa being steadily crushed by German forces slowly advancing through the countryside, with the natives resorting to <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_warfare" title="Guerrilla warfare">guerrilla warfare</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>German efforts to clear the bush of civilians in German South West Africa resulted in a genocide of the population. In total, as many as 65,000 Herero (80% of the total Herero population), and 10,000 Namaqua (50% of the total Namaqua population) either starved, died of thirst, or were worked to death in camps such as <a href="/wiki/Shark_Island_concentration_camp" title="Shark Island concentration camp">Shark Island concentration camp</a> between 1904 and 1908. Between 24,000 and 100,000 Hereros, 10,000 <a href="/wiki/Nama_people" title="Nama people">Nama</a>, and an unknown number of <a href="/wiki/San_people" title="San people">San</a> died in the genocide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walter_Nuhn_1904_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walter_Nuhn_1904-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sarkin_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-sarkin-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moses_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-moses-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Characteristic of this genocide was death from starvation, thirst, and possibly the poisoning of the population's wells, whilst they were trapped in the <a href="/wiki/Namib" title="Namib">Namib Desert</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-totten_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-totten-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kroll_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-kroll-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_consciousness_and_exhibitions">Colonial consciousness and exhibitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Colonial consciousness and exhibitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_lobby">Colonial lobby</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scramble_for_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Colonial lobby">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:African_Pigmies_CNE-v1-p58-B.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="1382" decoding="async" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/African_Pigmies_CNE-v1-p58-B.jpg/170px-African_Pigmies_CNE-v1-p58-B.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/African_Pigmies_CNE-v1-p58-B.jpg/255px-African_Pigmies_CNE-v1-p58-B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/African_Pigmies_CNE-v1-p58-B.jpg/340px-African_Pigmies_CNE-v1-p58-B.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:African_Pigmies_CNE-v1-p58-B.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pygmies" title="Pygmies">Pygmies</a> and a European. Some pygmies would be exposed in <a href="/wiki/Human_zoo" title="Human zoo">human zoos</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Ota_Benga" title="Ota Benga">Ota Benga</a> displayed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eugenicist" title="Eugenicist">eugenicist</a> <a href="/wiki/Madison_Grant" title="Madison Grant">Madison Grant</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Bronx_Zoo" title="Bronx Zoo">Bronx Zoo</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In its earlier stages, imperialism was generally the act of individual explorers as well as some adventurous merchantmen. The colonial powers were a long way from approving without any dissent the expensive adventures carried out abroad. Various important political leaders, such as <a href="/wiki/William_Ewart_Gladstone" title="William Ewart Gladstone">William Gladstone</a>, opposed colonization in its first years.
